    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";

            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||

              script.onreadystatechange = null;

            }
          };

          callback();

      }

    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2388372903365255005">In 19th-century Vienna, a drama of love, fate, and will is played out amid the intellectual ferment that defined the era.<br/><br/>Josef Breuer, one of the founding fathers of psychoanalysis, is at the height of his career. Friedrich Nietzsche, Europe's greatest philosopher, is on the brink of suicidal despair, unable to find a cure for the headaches and other ailments that plague h</span>
  <span id="freeText2388372903365255005" style="display:none">In 19th-century Vienna, a drama of love, fate, and will is played out amid the intellectual ferment that defined the era.<br /><br />Josef Breuer, one of the founding fathers of psychoanalysis, is at the height of his career. Friedrich Nietzsche, Europe's greatest philosopher, is on the brink of suicidal despair, unable to find a cure for the headaches and other ailments that plague him. When he agrees to treat Nietzsche with his experimental "talking cure", Breuer never expects that he, too, will find solace in their sessions. Only through facing his own inner demons can the gifted healer begin to help his patient.<br /><br />In When Nietzsche Wept, Irvin Yalom blends fact and fiction, atmosphere and suspense to unfold an unforgettable story about the redemptive power of friendship.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2388372903365255005" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });
      $('span_class_gr-hyperlink_sort_order_span_tip_988').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>    </div>

  <span id="freeText14099669578907215712" style="display:none">When Nietzsche wept, Irvin D. Yalom<br /><br />When Nietzsche Wept is a 1992 novel by Irvin D. Yalom. <br /><br />The novel starts with Dr. Josef Breuer, sitting in a cafe in Venice, Italy waiting for Lou Salomé, who was involved with Friedrich Nietzsche. <br /><br />She has written a letter stating that the future of the philosophy of Germany is at stake and that the German philosopher needs help desperately. <br /><br />The plot develops into a therapy where Breuer needs to have his soul treated, i.e. to help him get over a patient who he treated for hysteria and with whom he has fallen in love, whereas Nietzsche needs help with his migraines. <br /><br />Influenced by the revolutionary ideas of his young disciple Sigmund Freud, Josef Breuer starts the dangerous strategy that will mean the origin of the psychoanalysis. <div id="review_348754354" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/348754354" />
    <a title="Maxime" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4053015-maxime"><img alt="Maxime" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1309619101p2/4053015.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/348754354?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maxime" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maxime" href="/user/show/4053015-maxime">Maxime</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer348754354" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12493362814475379358">Wow. Simply fantastic. If you spend half as much time in your own head as I do mine, this might be the book for you. If you analyze yourself and your actions, enjoy the process of self discovery, or simply love psychology then I suggest you hit your nearest used book shop ASAP. This book makes no effort to hide its loftier aspects. I was not in the least surprised to learn that its author is a professor of psychology at Stanford and has written several text books on the subject. Yalom, however, </span>
  <span id="freeText12493362814475379358" style="display:none">Wow. Simply fantastic. If you spend half as much time in your own head as I do mine, this might be the book for you. If you analyze yourself and your actions, enjoy the process of self discovery, or simply love psychology then I suggest you hit your nearest used book shop ASAP. This book makes no effort to hide its loftier aspects. I was not in the least surprised to learn that its author is a professor of psychology at Stanford and has written several text books on the subject. Yalom, however, approaches his grandiose subject matter with such candor that each character seems to be himself written from a different angle as though their struggles were once his own. All inquiries of the self aside, this book is also a cool piece of historical fiction. For those who do not know, historical fiction is a book where the main characters, setting, and certain events may be historically accurate while the plot (or some part of it) is fictional. This book is a peek into what quite possibly might have gone through the minds of the renowned physician/psychologist Josef Breuer and philosopher Friedrich Nietzsche during the 1880s. Until reading the author's note at the end of the book I did not realize just how much of it was historically accurate. What makes this book so fascinating is that it wrestles with questions we all have about life, goes through the same arguments that we might, runs into the same dead ends, then refusing to take no for an answer dives deeper down the rabbit hole arriving at what I find to be some profound truths regarding love, life, and existence. Now I know not everyone will experience what I did reading this book so do not go reading it expecting to find the meaning of life or anything. But at the same time do not go reading it expecting not to find it either. At the very least you will find yourself pondering some classic philosophical questions while you observe the unfolding of a fascinating fictional psychology experiment. And if that sounds too exhausting, do not fret. Undoubtedly someone will make a lackluster film version within the decade. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12493362814475379358" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2147997232" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2147997232" />
    <a title="BlackOxford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford"><img alt="BlackOxford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484863606p2/17744555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2147997232?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BlackOxford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BlackOxford" href="/user/show/17744555-blackoxford">BlackOxford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17744555-blackoxford?shelf=philosophy-theology">philosophy-theology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2147997232" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer896776513133219231"><b>The Doctor of Despair</b><br/><br/>The <i>fin de siecle</i> Viennese satirist, Karl Kraus, took a dim view of the emerging field of psychiatry: “Psychoanalysis is that mental illness for which it regards itself as therapy.” And, somewhat surprisingly, this is the main theme of this novel by an eminent psychotherapist. Psychiatry is indeed a field of Byzantine relationships. Perhaps that is Yalom’s point.<br/><br/>Friedrich Nietzsche and Josef Breuer never really met; but Yalom puts them in an intense relationship of mutual th</span>
  <span id="freeText896776513133219231" style="display:none"><b>The Doctor of Despair</b><br /><br />The <i>fin de siecle</i> Viennese satirist, Karl Kraus, took a dim view of the emerging field of psychiatry: “Psychoanalysis is that mental illness for which it regards itself as therapy.” And, somewhat surprisingly, this is the main theme of this novel by an eminent psychotherapist. Psychiatry is indeed a field of Byzantine relationships. Perhaps that is Yalom’s point.<br /><br />Friedrich Nietzsche and Josef Breuer never really met; but Yalom puts them in an intense relationship of mutual therapy, each believing that the other is the patient and he the therapist. Breuer, Freud’s mentor and the discoverer of the psychoanalytic ‘talking cure’, is acutely depressed; Nietzsche, the as yet unknown philosopher, suffers from debilitating migraines. <br /><br />Nietzsche seeks to teach Breuer about ‘freedom’ by which he means a sort of resignation to one’s fate. Breuer sees his task as revealing Nietzsche’s emotional reality to himself. Neither succeeds. But in their failures they accomplish remarkable psychological things with themselves by trying to help the other. Breuer frees himself from his obsession with a patient and Nietzsche learns how to reduce the severity of his migraines.<br /><br />It appears, then, that Karl Kraus was on to something important as far as Yalom is concerned. Kraus summarised the situation thus: “My unconscious knows more about the consciousness of the psychologist than his consciousness knows about my unconscious.” Psychoanalysis is Byzantine indeed. Does anyone really understand its mechanism and effects? Yalom seems to doubt it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="896776513133219231" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/220023797" />
    <a title="Talia Carner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6302775-talia-carner"><img alt="Talia Carner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442455501p2/6302775.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/220023797?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Talia Carner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Talia Carner" href="/user/show/6302775-talia-carner">Talia Carner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780062009302" href="/book/show/8041691-when-nietzsche-wept">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer220023797" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10062462571352227164">I am not in the habit of trashing a book. However, since this novel had been published as a "Perennial Classic," there is no danger that I might destroy a writer's career. However, the choice of this book as a "classic" left me perplexed: why this book is held in such a high regard?<br/><br/>The premise of Yalom's novel is interesting: Get two of the most innovative thinkers of modern times to meet. And then what? Friederich Nietzsche and Josef Breuer--the father of modern philosophy and the father of mod</span>
  <span id="freeText10062462571352227164" style="display:none">I am not in the habit of trashing a book. However, since this novel had been published as a "Perennial Classic," there is no danger that I might destroy a writer's career. However, the choice of this book as a "classic" left me perplexed: why this book is held in such a high regard?<br /><br />The premise of Yalom's novel is interesting: Get two of the most innovative thinkers of modern times to meet. And then what? Friederich Nietzsche and Josef Breuer--the father of modern philosophy and the father of modern psychoanalysis (and a friend of the young Freud) had never met in life. But what if they had? <br /><br />I would have expected some new, explosive ideology to burst out of this fictional years-long relationship. Sort of 1 + 1 = 3. But this is not the case beyond some personal epiphanies. No new discipline emerges. <br /><br />Furthermore, in exploring each character, Yalom regurgitates their respective theories, while failing to move into the realm of new intellectual challenge either one of them might have presented to the other. Many of the "truths" the characters cite have, by now, become so common knowledge that they sound nonsensical. On rare occasions the novel brings some forgotten, interesting statement--mostly from Nietzsche--but not enough to spend time with 300 pages of talking heads. <br /><br />In the years I was honing my writing skills at writing workshops, not a single page of Yalom's dialogues would have passed a writing instructor's scrutiny. It would have been dismissed for its amateurishness, and the author would have been taught the basics of "show, don't tell." Yet, Yalom gives the reader a book-length uninterrupted flow of stilted lecturing. The dialogue lines lack tension, characterization--and certainly fail to move the story forward, which are the must ingredients for good, compelling dialogue. <br /><br />Which brings me to the underlining flaw of this work: The flimsy plot. In the first 100 pages, nothing happens from "scene" to "scene" because these are really not scenes, each with a beginning, a middle and an end, but rather sections of text. They lie flat, with little development other than more information parted to the reader. When things finally move, they are slow and predictable. <br /><br />A good novel can take the reader into a new world of ideology and intellectual thinking by placing the characters in situations where these theories can be examined under the pressure of events. It is called dramatization. It requires vivid imagination on the part of the writer, something Yalom clearly lacks.<br /><br />It is astonishing that Yalom's book has gone this far (reprinted by Harper Collins in their Perennial Classics series.) I hope that future writers do not take their cue from this dubious literary success. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10062462571352227164" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2119537644" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2119537644" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2119537644?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060975500" href="/book/show/753346.When_Nietzsche_Wept">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=what-the">what-the</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2119537644" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12489247194272407891">Reading this and looking at some of the other titles Yalom has published the suspicion grew that he might like to write a series of adventures about an enigmatic doctor who travels through time and space solving complex mysteries. Of course ideally this doctor would need some kind of charming but hapless companion, in this case the young Freud whose suggestions nearly lead Dr. Breuer into disaster but who eventually turns good thanks to his handy use of a watch to hypnotise the good Doctor into </span>
  <span id="freeText12489247194272407891" style="display:none">Reading this and looking at some of the other titles Yalom has published the suspicion grew that he might like to write a series of adventures about an enigmatic doctor who travels through time and space solving complex mysteries. Of course ideally this doctor would need some kind of charming but hapless companion, in this case the young Freud whose suggestions nearly lead Dr. Breuer into disaster but who eventually turns good thanks to his handy use of a watch to hypnotise the good Doctor into experiencing a parallel life from which he can emerge thinking <i>Maya, maya</i> and thus with new understanding accept and embrace his life, ah...<br /><br />The title promises tears, cruelly the author holds them back to the last page, whether his technique in this regard is equal to that of noted Northumbrian wordsmith <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/83837.Catherine_Cookson" title="Catherine Cookson" rel="nofollow noopener"> Catherine Cookson</a> I don't know, nor am I much moved to find out.<br /><br />As I noted in the updates I started this book in the expectation that it would be awful, hopefully amusingly so, at first say for about a hundred pages I was obliged to channel the spirit of Robin the boy wonder, and exclaim every other paragraph or so <i> "Holy info-dump Batman!"</i> the interesting thing here was that the information was irrelevant - does it matter if Lou Salome crossed her legs or not, or what shape Dr. Breuer's breakfast roll was, or if he ate one or three, or if Neitzsche met a Buddhist monk, any monk or just read about one in Switzerland, or what Yalom believes a typical upper middle-class Viennese dinner in 1882 consisted of, or who ate whipped cream with their cake?<br /><br />Yalom's triangular <i>Kaisersemmel</i> gave me traumatic flashbacks to reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22041082.All_the_Light_We_Cannot_See" title="All the Light We Cannot See by Anthony Doerr" rel="nofollow noopener"> All the light we cannot see</a> and left me thinking if you are just going to make stuff up you might as well have the inter-galactic space dog leave off chasing the Rings of Saturn and come down to cure Nietzsche, admittedly since an inter-galactic space dog is still a dog, the cure would consist of urging Nietsche to put on his hover boots and come down the park to chase squirrels, but hey, why not? Woof! Woof!<br /><br />Well, one reason would be is that this is all a bit pedagogical and space dogs generally are not on hand to assist people studying to become therapists to help their clients <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and mores the pity, though the squirrels are awfully relieved <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, I sense in this book there is an attempt to communicate to students some of the difficulties and pitfalls of working with a client. Personally if I was one of those students I think I would prefer the blurry photostat with the twelve bullet-points ,but no doubt I'm just grumpy, or old fashioned, or not up for monetising my experience through a new medium or something.<br /><br />I'm mildly interested that English is unusually not the dominant choice of language for reviewing this book. Which reminds me of George Mikes' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13326372.How_to_Date_an_Alien__My_Alien_Romance___1_" title="How to Date an Alien (My Alien Romance, #1) by Magan Vernon" rel="nofollow noopener">How to be an alien</a> in which he mentions that a French book about the Popular Front was mistranslated into English as the Popular Behind and became a best selling marital aid. I wonder if this novel was translated back into English if we'd find that Herr Dr. Breuer was powerfully aroused by the touch of Herr Professor Nietzsche's famously droopy moustache and that he urged his darling Fritzchen not to forget his whip the next time he came? Stranger things have happened I imagine <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ but not triangular Kaisersemmel, that is too much <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Still there is something nice and genuinely touching <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ not in a creepy way like a predatory employer <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> in people wanting to read and indeed write about dead people like Nietzsche, Breuer and Freud rather than mysteriously topless muscular firemen or space-dogs or treasure hunting Nazis and what have you. It suggests some kind of hunger for knowledge and understanding, even a desire for engagement with big ideas and fancy notions. Whether books like this are the gateway drugs to harder stuff or an end in themselves I couldn't say <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ because I don't know <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ at a guess both probably <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. On the other hand this is absolutely the book for you if you've ever wanted to eavesdrop on somebody else's therapy sessions without having to plant a bug in the room. Although it may leave you with the desire to burst in with a jar of wasabi paste and a spoon.<br /><br />Final thought. One character urges the other to consider if his tears had voices, what would they say? Hard not to say 'give me a tissue you bastard, can't you see I'm crying'?</span>
  <a data-text-id="12489247194272407891" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13049441842470786578">Beach reading for the brainy set. Keeping in mind that this is one of Yalom’s “teaching novels,” envisioned not to entertain, or even to achieve artistically, but to serve as a type of literary experiential learning tool for therapists and therapists-to-be, really helps with tolerating the expository nature of much of the book. Also, Yalom’s nerdy and passionate enthusiasm is infectious, and if one surrenders to it, it allows the reader to join in with the fun he clearly was having writing this </span>
  <span id="freeText13049441842470786578" style="display:none">Beach reading for the brainy set. Keeping in mind that this is one of Yalom’s “teaching novels,” envisioned not to entertain, or even to achieve artistically, but to serve as a type of literary experiential learning tool for therapists and therapists-to-be, really helps with tolerating the expository nature of much of the book. Also, Yalom’s nerdy and passionate enthusiasm is infectious, and if one surrenders to it, it allows the reader to join in with the fun he clearly was having writing this imagined relationship, strengthening the ideological connection he swears by between psychotherapy and existentialism.</span>

<div id="review_3235795" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3235795" />
    <a title="Connie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/202710-connie"><img alt="Connie" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3235795?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Connie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Connie" href="/user/show/202710-connie">Connie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3235795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13718311646201618326">In this amazing novel Yalom blends philosophy, psychoanalysis and history and imagines what would have happened had Nietzsche gone into therapy with Breuer. The plot thrives on the tensions that arise between Nietzsche's nihilistic philosophy and Freud's belief in the fundamental role of relationships on human life and development. This book is an emotional and intellectual tour de force and brings to life two of the most magnificent thinkers of the 20th century. Yalom at his best! </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_28669926" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/28669926" />
    <a title="♥ Ibrahim ♥ " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1178748-ibrahim"><img alt="♥ Ibrahim ♥ " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557598809p2/1178748.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/28669926?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="♥ Ibrahim ♥ " class="user" itemprop="url" name="♥ Ibrahim ♥ " href="/user/show/1178748-ibrahim">♥ Ibrahim ♥ </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1178748-ibrahim?shelf=thinkers-i-adore">thinkers-i-adore</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1178748-ibrahim?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer28669926" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3915858506754021132">Thanks to this book, I have grown to love, respect and have a tender spot in my heart for Nietzsche. Based on the infamous quote "God is Dead" I always took him to be an arrogant man who didn't know what he was talking about. A book like this helps me take Nietzsche in his own context. It is so easy to read, fun, intellectually stimulating, and I keep feeling that I am getting more and more educated. Nietzsche had such a gentle soul but in his books he sounded shrill, often combative. How is tha</span>
  <span id="freeText3915858506754021132" style="display:none">Thanks to this book, I have grown to love, respect and have a tender spot in my heart for Nietzsche. Based on the infamous quote "God is Dead" I always took him to be an arrogant man who didn't know what he was talking about. A book like this helps me take Nietzsche in his own context. It is so easy to read, fun, intellectually stimulating, and I keep feeling that I am getting more and more educated. Nietzsche had such a gentle soul but in his books he sounded shrill, often combative. How is that? Well, it's no great mystery. If no one will listen, it's only natural to shout (p.195). He wrote for us, for you and me, as his philosophy was about life and for life. This reminds me of why Sartre also wrote books of philosophy when he never intended to philosophize, per se; he only was writing about  as we generally live it.<br /><br />From chapter 15 onwards, Nietzsche is an outstanding psychotherapist who knows the ins and outs of human soul and helps Dr Breuer with his despair. They swap roles and the doctor here is the patient. Nietzsche's writing succeeds not because he is intelligent or scholarly but because he has the daring to detach himself from the comfort of the herd and face strong and evil inclinations, and what doesn't kill him will make him stronger as usual. <br /><br />The most important lesson that I am learning from this book, what is sticking in my brain and I would love to keep remembering it is what Nietzsche is advising Dr Breur to do:Be a man and do not follow me-but yourself. But yourself! Here he is warning against subordination to people and that we should copycat nobody. To each is their own path. Follow your own calling. Call no man a father. My way might be perfect for me, but it is not necessarily perfect for you as well. The best I can do is to help you find your own way, but never to encourage you to blindly find my way. Become yourself. Stick to who you are. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3915858506754021132" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1413743946" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1413743946" />
    <a title="Laysee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee"><img alt="Laysee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459741824p2/4863523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1413743946?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laysee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laysee" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee">Laysee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4863523-laysee?shelf=five-star-books">five-star-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1413743946" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7946024418645518750"><br/>"Fiction is history that might have happened." - Irvin D. Yalom<br/><br/>"When Neitzsche Wept" is a phenomenal novel that creatively marries fact and fiction. It fires the imagination even as it simultaneously engages both the heart and mind. <br/><br/>Its author, Irvin Yalom, an American existential psychiatrist, intended it as a teaching novel. However, it did not read like one. How ingenious to create a pedagogical device "to introduce the student to the fundamentals of existential therapy"!<br/><br/>At center stage were </span>
  <span id="freeText7946024418645518750" style="display:none"><br />"Fiction is history that might have happened." - Irvin D. Yalom<br /><br />"When Neitzsche Wept" is a phenomenal novel that creatively marries fact and fiction. It fires the imagination even as it simultaneously engages both the heart and mind. <br /><br />Its author, Irvin Yalom, an American existential psychiatrist, intended it as a teaching novel. However, it did not read like one. How ingenious to create a pedagogical device "to introduce the student to the fundamentals of existential therapy"!<br /><br />At center stage were three well known historical professionals whose lives interacted. They were:<br />1. Josef Breuer: An Austrian physician and researcher investigator of respiration and equilibrium. He was an outstanding diagnostician who tended to the elite in Vienna. He developed the talking cure in his seminal work with his patient, fictitiously named Anna O. Breuer was presented as supremely competent but overworked, over-involved with his patients, in despair, and needed help. <br /><br />2. Sigmund Freud: Breuer's protégé, a struggling young intern who had to decide between a clinical or research career. Breuer sought his counsel often in his treatment of non-medical cases. <br /><br />3. Friedrich Neitzsche: a young and brilliant thinker in philology. A guarded social recluse, he suffered from severe migraine, was depressed and suicidal, but would not remotely consider getting help for his dark moods. <br /><br />Yalom grounded their life circumstances in this fictional novel in fact. Whole and parts of letters quoted were alleged to be authentic. In 1882, psychotherapy was not yet born but fascinating developments were beginning to emerge in treating psychological conditions. Freud and Breuer's "Studies on Hysteria" subsequently launched the psychoanalytic revolution. <br /><br />I was impressed by how remarkably this novel was crafted. The novel offered a wealth of information on the practice of medicine in the 19th century, the emergence of psychoanalysis, hypnosis, and the "talking therapy" of the Angst Doctor. <br /><br />For me, much of the novel's appeal came from shadowing Breuer while he stretched the limits of his competencies to help Neitzsche while appearing not to do so. The 38-year-old Neitzsche was a patient from hell. He was fiercely intelligent and very formidable. He was proud of his courage to have black moods. He denied being suicidal. <br /><br />Breuer too was battling marital woes and an obsession with a former seductive patient (Bertha Pappenheim, a.k.a Anna O). He had a predilection for beautiful women; hence he could not refuse the invitation extended by the stunning but impertinent Russian beauty, Lou Salome, to treat Neitzsche whose heart she had broken. The twists and turns that led to how a mutual contract was struck between Breuer and Neitzsche to serve as each other's therapist were riveting. <br /><br />Their mutual treatment led to a prolonged and profound discussion on the compatibility of searching for peace and truth concurrently. The scintillating sparring between Breuer and Neitzsche was most invigorating and entertaining, akin to listening to two masters play verbal chess. Neitzsche's nihilistic views on a host of issues were persuasive and disturbing at the same time. Neitzsche's take on truth: "It is not the truth that is holy, but the search for one's own truth! Can there be a more sacred act than self-inquiry?" How does one argue against this?<br /><br />Breuer, the physician, opined that "Above all, hope must be preserved. And who else but the physician can sustain hope?" He could not bear breaking bad news to his patients. Neitzsche, the philosopher, had an opposing view: "Hope? Hope is the final evil." "Hope is the worst of evils because it protracts torment." Neitzsche believed, and I agree, that a physician ought to prepare a patient for death. <br /><br />I found Neitzsche's views appealing in startling ways. However, his philosophy about life ("a great dice game"), God ("the everlastingly bloated nipple"), mortality, marriage, duty, and faithfulness was very hard to swallow. He was right in proclaiming himself "a teacher of bitter truths, an unpopular prophet".<br /><br />Neitzsche emerged as the more dominant therapist and in actuality, a wretched one. He was incapable of connecting socially and empathically. He was dismissive of Breuer's pain. It was almost hilarious reading counsel that went like this: "Of course, you are fearful, living means to be in danger. Grow hard!... You are not a cow, and I am no apostle of cud chewing." Not funny.<br /><br />How both Breuer and Neitzsche desperately sought liberation from their obsession kept me reading. A couple of times along this journey with them, I was close to tears. The treatment attested to the power of the subconscious mind and the importance of the therapeutic relationship. <br /><br />But above all, what moved me most deeply was the trust, honesty, and friendship that Breuer and Neitzsche forged in the fiery dungeon of their despair and that emerged like refined gold. <br /><br />When Neitzsche wept, I believe he wept for all of us because like him, we are human too, "all too human".<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7946024418645518750" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1879918775" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1879918775" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1879918775?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=philo-psychol">philo-psychol</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=life-stages">life-stages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2017-read">2017-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=medical">medical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=bio">bio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=austria">austria</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1879918775" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5346807894609957182">In reading this book I was hoping to better understand the German existentialist philosopher Friedrich Wilhelm Nietzsche (1844 - 1900), both the man and his particular form of existentialism. The book takes place in the last months of 1882 when Nietzsche was 38 years old; at this date he was neither widely read nor publicly acclaimed as he posthumously came to be. Some of the events of the story did not happen, but they very easily <b>could</b> have happened, this being a definition of fiction put fort</span>
  <span id="freeText5346807894609957182" style="display:none">In reading this book I was hoping to better understand the German existentialist philosopher Friedrich Wilhelm Nietzsche (1844 - 1900), both the man and his particular form of existentialism. The book takes place in the last months of 1882 when Nietzsche was 38 years old; at this date he was neither widely read nor publicly acclaimed as he posthumously came to be. Some of the events of the story did not happen, but they very easily <b>could</b> have happened, this being a definition of fiction put forth by André Gide, a French author and winner of the 1947 Nobel Prize in Literature. It is just such fiction that Irvin D. Yalom wished to write. <br /><br />That what happens in the story could have happened, even if it did not happen, is explained in a well written and very interesting afterword. Letters are quoted that give proof of the possibility of the fictitious events. What is true and what is not is clarified, even if at times it can be hard to keep straight. The author goes on to explain why he wrote the book, what he wished to achieve by it and why in detail he drew the story as he did. On completion of the lengthy afterword I much better understood the book - why he chose the year 1882,why he chose to incorporate particular real-life characters and why he chose to fabricate the fictional elements as he did. On completing the afterword my whole view of the book changed; only then did I come to <b>really</b> like the book. Nevertheless, I have chosen to give the book three rather than four stars because I do not think a book should have to be explained to be appreciated. That made clear in the afterword should have been conveyed in the story itself. My interpretation of the story before reading the afterword and after reading it were different. Without the afterword I wouldn’t have considered giving the book more than three stars. The bottom line is that I would probably have preferred that the author had presented the figures, their beliefs and actions in a straightforward book of non-fiction. <br /><br />It is important to know that the author is an accomplished psychotherapist, an existentialist and an Emeritus Professor of Psychiatry at Stanford University. He writes about that which he knows. <br /><br />The author saw the book as an implement for teaching. I did learn elements of Nietzsche's philosophy. While the basic tenets of his philosophy are made clear, an in-depth analysis of the philosophy requires a different book. One’s appetite is merely whetted and there is a lot of repetition. <br /><br />The two central figures are Nietzsche and Joseph Breuer (1842 – 1925), the latter being a prominent Austrian physician working in the field of neurophysiology. Breuer had from 1880 – 1882 been using talk therapy to treat Bertha Pappenheim (1859 – 1936) for hysteria, an illness which at this time was coupled to the female sex. Sigmund Freud (1856 – 1939) and Breuer came to write about her under the pseudonym “Anna O.” in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/249041.Studies_in_Hysteria" title="Studies in Hysteria by Josef Breuer" rel="nofollow noopener">Studies in Hysteria</a>. Published in 1895, it is considered one of the founding texts of psychoanalysis. We also know that Breuer’s wife had become jealous of her husband's interest in Bertha, and rumors had begun to circulate. All of the above is true. <br /><br />Breuer is 40 in 1882, and being 40 he <b>could</b> have had of a mid-life crisis. This is how the story is drawn here - he became infatuated with his patient, he ignored his wife, he couldn’t resist the flirtatious requests of Lou Salomé. Lou Salomé <b>did</b> actually exist and she <b>was</b> having a “relation à trois” with Nietzsche and Paul Rée. Nietzsche <b>did</b> propose marriage to her, but she refused. In the story Lou requests that Breuer treat Nietzsche. Perhaps, perhaps, it might have been so, but we do know that Breuer never did meet Nietzsche. The ins and outs, the true facts and the possibilities become confusing. <br /><br />Before reading the afterword, what I saw was a book about a mid-life crisis and the book was more about Breuer than Nietzsche! We are told of Nietzsche debilitating illness, both physical and psychological, but his illness doesn’t become palpable until the very end. Only at the end does the focus shift to Nietzsche, and only then did I feel <b>his</b> suffering. For me he was the stronger of the two men. <br /><br />I feel some aspects of the fictional story lacked credibility. In 1882 psychotherapy had not yet come into practice and it was never practiced by Nietzsche. Is it conceivable that the distinguished physician Breuer would treat <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Nietzsche’s physical disorders <b>in exchange for</b> advice from Nietzsche to help Breuer overcome his despair, his fear of death and his erotic fixation for Bertha? I found the rapid resolution of Breuer’s marital discord not credibly drawn.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The author wants to show that much of the acclaim given to Freud rightly belongs to Nietzsche. In the book he has the younger Freud influenced by Nietzsche through Breuer, but we know that in reality they never met! <br /><br />The author wants to show and does show how existentialism could be used in psychotherapy. He wants to show and does show how both patient and therapist influence each other. The fictional aspects of the story convey this message. <br /><br />The audiobook is narrated by Paul Michael Garcia. He uses a stronger, firmer intonation for Breuer’s words. Nietzsche’s intonation is weaker, more questioning, less firm. Should it be this way? As stated above, only by the book’s end did I feel Nietzsche’s suffering so for most of the book the intonations felt wrong. On the other hand I could hear who was speaking by the use of two intonations, and this was certainly helpful. <br /><br />The book well draws Vienna in the 1880s and the birth of psychotherapy. <br /><br />Are you curious about existentialism? I highly recommend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25658482.At_the_Existentialist_Caf__Freedom__Being__and_Apricot_Cocktails" title="At the Existentialist Café Freedom, Being, and Apricot Cocktails by Sarah Bakewell" rel="nofollow noopener">At the Existentialist Café: Freedom, Being, and Apricot Cocktails</a>. Unfortunately it does not cover Nietzsche.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5346807894609957182" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_84390816" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/84390816" />
    <a title="Bob Nichols" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2105523-bob-nichols"><img alt="Bob Nichols" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314751863p2/2105523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/84390816?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Nichols" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Nichols" href="/user/show/2105523-bob-nichols">Bob Nichols</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060975500" href="/book/show/753346.When_Nietzsche_Wept">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer84390816" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16971233919323796710">Similar to his novel on Schopenhauer, Yallom's novel on Nietzsche illustrates the cathartic value of the "talking cure" approach used in psychotherapy. Yallom sets the stage (early 1880s, Vienna) by using two historic figures, Dr. Joseph Breuer (who is associated with laying the foundation of psychoanalysis) and the philosopher Nietzsche, both of whom were afflicted with obsessive love interests with two beautiful women. In the background, the young Freud serves as a sounding board for Breuer.<br/><br/>Ya</span>
  <span id="freeText16971233919323796710" style="display:none">Similar to his novel on Schopenhauer, Yallom's novel on Nietzsche illustrates the cathartic value of the "talking cure" approach used in psychotherapy. Yallom sets the stage (early 1880s, Vienna) by using two historic figures, Dr. Joseph Breuer (who is associated with laying the foundation of psychoanalysis) and the philosopher Nietzsche, both of whom were afflicted with obsessive love interests with two beautiful women. In the background, the young Freud serves as a sounding board for Breuer.<br /><br />Yallom uses Nietzsche's philosophy of freedom - generally, living for oneself, not others; particularly, freedom from his sexual attachment toward one of his patients - to push Breuer to his breaking point. Only then, through a hypnotic session with Freud, does Breuer come to terms with his obsession that represented his loss of a freedom to live as he really would like to live without regard for what others thought. With this, Breuer becomes a free man to re-engage life on his own terms.<br /><br />Despite Nietzsche's strong push of Breuer, it turns out that Nietzsche was not following his own counsel and his overarching philosophy of freedom. Nietzsche's sexual obsession pulls him down, but his more chronic problem is his own fear about exposing his imperfection (his sexual lust) and more generally opening up himself to others. Breuer busts through Nietzsche's defenses here and forces him to talk about what he had for so long bottled up inside. Nietzsche opens up. He weeps and "gives his tears a voice," and regains his energy for his life which he then applies to his seminal work, "Thus Spoke Zarathustra."<br /><br />This is a fun novel, even though Yallom appropriates Nietzsche to illustrate the value of talking, hypnosis, and cathartic release. This image is at odds with Nietzsche's fiercely-worded philosophy that has little place for the traditional sentiments of human kind. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16971233919323796710" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1787743710" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1787743710" />
    <a title="Marina (Sonnenbarke)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7800917-marina-sonnenbarke"><img alt="Marina (Sonnenbarke)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329525124p2/7800917.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1787743710?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marina (Sonnenbarke)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marina (Sonnenbarke)" href="/user/show/7800917-marina-sonnenbarke">Marina (Sonnenbarke)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788854501638" href="/book/show/9850664-le-lacrime-di-nietzsche">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7800917-marina-sonnenbarke?shelf=_literary-fiction">_literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7800917-marina-sonnenbarke?shelf=usa">usa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1787743710" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8326396395319655785">For a review in Italian, please visit: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://sonnenbarke.wordpress.com/2017/04/17/irvin-d-yalom-le-lacrime-di-nietzsche/'>https://sonnenbarke.wordpress.com/201...</a><br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/909675.Irvin_D__Yalom' title='Irvin D. Yalom' rel='nofollow noopener'>Irvin D. Yalom</a> is a famous American psychiatrist and psychotherapist, who writes with this book a novel about the history of psychotherapy. So he must know what he's writing about. It is important to keep in mind that this is a novel and not a non-fiction book. At the end of the book the author adds a note where he explains which parts are fiction and which are factual. This is very useful in order to better understan</span>
  <span id="freeText8326396395319655785" style="display:none">For a review in Italian, please visit: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://sonnenbarke.wordpress.com/2017/04/17/irvin-d-yalom-le-lacrime-di-nietzsche/">https://sonnenbarke.wordpress.com/201...</a><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/909675.Irvin_D__Yalom" title="Irvin D. Yalom" rel="nofollow noopener">Irvin D. Yalom</a> is a famous American psychiatrist and psychotherapist, who writes with this book a novel about the history of psychotherapy. So he must know what he's writing about. It is important to keep in mind that this is a novel and not a non-fiction book. At the end of the book the author adds a note where he explains which parts are fiction and which are factual. This is very useful in order to better understand what he's writing about.<br /><br />We are in 1882. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Josef_Breuer" rel="nofollow noopener">Joseph Breuer</a> is a famous physician working in Vienna, who is at present on holiday with his wife in Venice. He receives a note from a certain <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lou_Andreas-Salom%C3%A9" rel="nofollow noopener">Lou Salomé</a>, who wants to meet him because the future of philosophy is at stake. Her friend <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" rel="nofollow noopener">Friedrich Nietzsche</a> is severely depressed, with suicidal ideation. Lou wants for Breuer to take him on as his patient, and that is because he had a patient, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bertha_Pappenheim" rel="nofollow noopener">Bertha Pappenheim</a>, known to his students as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anna_O." rel="nofollow noopener">Anna O.</a>, whom he treated with talk therapy, a new kind of therapy invented by him, which consisted in hypnotizing the patient and letting her talk. Breuer, fascinated by Lou Salomé, decides to take on the patient, who is going to be a very difficult one, since he doesn't want to be cured.<br /><br />Joseph Breuer was an actual Austrian doctor, famous in real life for treating Bertha Pappenheim with talk therapy. Bertha, who suffers from <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3480686/" rel="nofollow noopener">hysteria</a>, will become Anna O. in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" rel="nofollow noopener">Sigmund Freud</a>'s book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/249041.Studies_in_Hysteria" title="Studies in Hysteria by Josef Breuer" rel="nofollow noopener">Studies in Hysteria</a>, written together with Breuer. Freud is also a character in the book, portrayed as a young medicine student and a friend of the Breuers' family. Lou Salomé was a writer and psychoanalyst of Russian origin, known for a series of friendships and relationships with various famous characters, most notably <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rainer_Maria_Rilke" rel="nofollow noopener">Rainer Maria Rilke</a> (who is not in Yalom's story). Friedrich Nietzsche doesn't need an introduction, but let me just say that in this novel he is a 38-year-old unknow philosopher who has written two books that almost nobody has read, namely <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/451565.Human__All_Too_Human" title="Human, All Too Human by Friedrich Nietzsche" rel="nofollow noopener">Human, All Too Human</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/94578.The_Gay_Science" title="The Gay Science by Friedrich Nietzsche" rel="nofollow noopener">The Gay Science</a>. He never actually got to meet Joseph Breuer in real life, but he really was in love with Lou Salomé and proposed to her, who refused him.<br /><br />The story is well-written and might appeal not only to psychotherapists or those interested in the history of psychotherapy, but also to all those who like a good, solid story which is engaging and well-written. It does have some minor flaws (for instance (BIG spoiler ahead), when <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Breuer "comes back" from his hypnotic experience with Freud and finds out he really is in love with his wife – she never for a moment thinks his renewed love for her to be weird and out of character, since he had not loved her one bit so far, at least for the last several months <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>), but all in all I consider it to be a good novel, which I wouldn't hesistate to recommend to my friends who love good literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8326396395319655785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1681012586" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1681012586" />
    <a title="Shaimaa Ali" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4176602-shaimaa-ali"><img alt="Shaimaa Ali" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573916463p2/4176602.jpg" /></a>

  <span id="freeText5023078166703351605" style="display:none">I raised my case .. 5/5 and straight to my favorite shelf :-)<br /><br />Excerpts:<br />--------<br />Page 82:<br />"My whole life has become a journey, and I begin to feel that my only home, the only familiar place to which I always return, is my illness.”<br /><br />P103 : <br />Is it my duty to impose a truth on others that they do not wish to know?"<br /> “Who can determine what one wishes not to know?” Nietzsche demanded.<br /><br />P109: <br />Dying is hard. I’ve always felt the final reward of the dead is to die no more!”<br /><br />P141: <br />You wonder about a conversation with nothing concealed—its real name is hell, I believe. To disclose oneself to another is the prelude to betrayal, and betrayal makes one sick, does it not?”.<br /><br />P210: <br />The other day you described your belief that the specter of nihilism was stalking Europe. You argued that Darwin has made God obsolete, that just as we once created God, we have all now killed him. And that we no longer know how to live without our religious mythologies. Now I know you didn’t say this directly—correct me if I’m mistaken—but I believe you consider it your mission to demonstrate that out of disbelief one can create a code of behavior for man, a new morality, a new enlightenment, to replace one born out of superstition and the lust for the supernatural.” He paused.<br /><br />P256:<br />After twenty years of such wondering, I now believe that fears are not born of darkness; rather, fears are like the stars—always there, but obscured by the glare of daylight. And dreams, dreams are a glorious mystery which beg to be understood. I envy you your dreams. I rarely capture mine.<br /><br />P265:<br />In that letter, I stated that there was a basic division of the ways of men: those who wish for peace of soul and happiness must believe and embrace faith, while those who wish to pursue the truth must forsake peace of mind and devote their life to live inquiry. <br /><br />P282:<br />“The fact that the will cannot will backward does not mean the will is impotent! Because, thank God, God is dead—that does not mean existence has no purpose! Because death comes—that does not mean that life has no value. These are all things I shall teach you in time.<br /><br />P341:<br />But who will protect us—the holy skeptics? Who will warn us of threats to the love of wisdom and hatred of servitude? Shall that be my calling? We skeptics have our enemies, our Satans who undermine our doubting and plant the seeds of faith in the most cunning places. Thus we kill gods, but we sanctify their replacements—teachers, artists, beautiful women. <br /><br />P346:<br />Though no stones hear and none can see<br />Each sobs softly, ‘Remember me. Remember me.'<br /><br />P414:<br />“I don’t know what else to say except that, thanks to you, I know that the key to living well is first to will that which is necessary and then to love that which is willed."<br /><br />P432:<br />“Do you know that no other woman has ever touched me? Not to be loved or touched-ever? To live an absolutely unobserved life-do you know what that is like? Often I go for days without saying a word to anyone, except perhaps ”Guten Morgen’ and “Guten Abend’to my Gasthaus owner. Yes, Josef, you were right in your interpretation of ‘no slot.’ I belong nowhere. I have no home, no circle of friends to whom I speak daily, no closet full of belongings, no family hearth. I don’t even have a state, for I have given up my German citizenship and never remain in one place long enough to get a Swiss passport.”<br /><br />P437:<br />"Perhaps we’re all fellow sufferers unable to see each other’s truth.”<br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2129965598" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10500730885939022728">Nietzsche was anything but one dimensional as this novel tries to make him. There is a reason why all 20th century philosophy went through Nietzsche in one way or another. This book and this author have no idea why and clearly they only understand Nietzsche as if he were a comic book character. He's not. Aphorisms are a dangerous thing to quote out of context. This author does that and tries to make a whole book out of muddled psychoanalytical babble with a weak story melding a shallow plot. <br/><br/>I'm</span>
  <span id="freeText10500730885939022728" style="display:none">Nietzsche was anything but one dimensional as this novel tries to make him. There is a reason why all 20th century philosophy went through Nietzsche in one way or another. This book and this author have no idea why and clearly they only understand Nietzsche as if he were a comic book character. He's not. Aphorisms are a dangerous thing to quote out of context. This author does that and tries to make a whole book out of muddled psychoanalytical babble with a weak story melding a shallow plot. <br /><br />I'm grateful to be out of the 90s (this book was originally published 1992) and this book and the shallowness shown with this book shows the zeitgeist of a time that is best forgotten. <br /><br />Don't be afraid to read Freud or Nietzsche directly. They really aren't that difficult to follow and a book like this one has too many errors of thought and is best left unread. (that's a note to my future self and hopefully will serve as a warning to me if I ever pick up this time waster of a book in a parallel universe).</span>

<div id="review_562532" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/562532" />
    <a title="khashayar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8275-khashayar"><img alt="khashayar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280345967p2/8275.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/562532?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="khashayar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="khashayar" href="/user/show/8275-khashayar">khashayar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer562532" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8292621328415568289">Too much fiction, too little philosophy. Ironically, somewhere in the book Nietzsche abhors the idea of reading the recast philosophy. That's why he learned Greek. Drinking from the source spring rather than the still water in the carafe, he learned to read the firsthand writings and then the philologer became a philosopher. Keeping this point in your mind, imagine if you can learn Nietzsche's philosophy by reading merely the excerpts of his early works quoted now and then in the text.<br/><br/>Even so, t</span>
  <span id="freeText8292621328415568289" style="display:none">Too much fiction, too little philosophy. Ironically, somewhere in the book Nietzsche abhors the idea of reading the recast philosophy. That's why he learned Greek. Drinking from the source spring rather than the still water in the carafe, he learned to read the firsthand writings and then the philologer became a philosopher. Keeping this point in your mind, imagine if you can learn Nietzsche's philosophy by reading merely the excerpts of his early works quoted now and then in the text.<br /><br />Even so, the book is interesting and has handful of surprises. The idea of bringing Nietzsche and Breuer together and setting a novel is certainly appealing. Furthermore, the quoted aphorisms are indeed intriguing and somehow weakly philosophical. I also admit that every iconoclast is still a human: same passion, same points of weakness. One should not expect to encounter superheros (ubermenschen) in the novel, even if the main characters are indeed intellectual giants.<br /><br />The presented topics and subjects, namely:<br /><br />a) the love-triangle of Lou, Fritz, and Paul and how this relationship- once a Pythagorean as Nietzsche in former times named it- afflicts the philosopher,<br /><br />b) the nightmares of Breuer, Freud's tutor,<br /><br />c) Lou's weltanschauung, etc.<br /><br />are also interesting.<br /><br />However, I find the book an average one. Perhaps, the book could be more impressive if the author had chosen a humbler title instead of what he finally came up with. The present title of the book, i.e. 'When Nietzsche Wept', sounds too strong. Although, I might be the one who expects too much of such a title and this might be my own illusion. Regardless of this, there is still one objective point of weakness in the book, viz. Nietzsche's ridiculous and banal remedial processes as they appear in the midst of the novel. This part is certainly out of the place.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8292621328415568289" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3274297271" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3274297271" />
    <a title="Philippe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4903281-philippe"><img alt="Philippe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422300629p2/4903281.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3274297271?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philippe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philippe" href="/user/show/4903281-philippe">Philippe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4903281-philippe?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4903281-philippe?shelf=nietzsche">nietzsche</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4903281-philippe?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4903281-philippe?shelf=personal-development">personal-development</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4903281-philippe?shelf=1860-1918">1860-1918</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3274297271" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15773300682331538713">I approached this book with considerable doubt. For decades, Nietzsche has been a mentor to me and the tenor of his life and works have deeply impregnated my worldview. So, one is bound to be disappointed by novelistic impersonations of this complex and demanding human being, thinker, poet. Particularly when its title already suggests emotional entanglements and melodrama. My initial impressions seemed to validate these expectations. The first few chapters struck me as rather cartoonish and I wa</span>
  <span id="freeText15773300682331538713" style="display:none">I approached this book with considerable doubt. For decades, Nietzsche has been a mentor to me and the tenor of his life and works have deeply impregnated my worldview. So, one is bound to be disappointed by novelistic impersonations of this complex and demanding human being, thinker, poet. Particularly when its title already suggests emotional entanglements and melodrama. My initial impressions seemed to validate these expectations. The first few chapters struck me as rather cartoonish and I was ready to put the book aside. But once Lou Salomé disappears from the scene and the interaction between the two protagonists gets going, I became very involved in the story. Yalom did a wonderful job in painting a sober, but to my mind truthful portrait of Nietzsche. It has just the right texture, an intriguing mix of intellectual acuity and spiritual magnanimity. His counterpart, a Jewish medical doctor Josef Breuer, is no less compelling. The book reads like a transcript of a series of 'existential psychotherapy' sessions in which foundational concerns about mortality, purpose, freedom and relationships with other human beings are played out against the background of a rather accurate rendering of Nietzsche's mature philosophy. The story's unity of time and place and its fidelity to quasi-historical circumstances (it's history that <i>might</i> have happened) heighten the reading pleasure. And, yes, Nietzsche's tears were totally justified.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15773300682331538713" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
